<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Language group of the Sinitic languages</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the Sinitic languages excluding the Greater Bai languages. For other languages in China, see <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_China" title="Languages of China">Languages of China</a>. For the standard language, see <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Chinese</a>. For different varieties, see <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese" title="Varieties of Chinese">Varieties of Chinese</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Unless otherwise specified, Chinese in this article is written in <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified</a>/<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional</a>/<a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a> order. If the simplified and traditional characters are the same, they are written only once.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

Pinyin</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Chinese-language romanization"><span lang="zh-Latn" style="font-style: normal">jhong-wún</span></span></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" Min</a></th></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Hokkien" title="Hokkien">Hokkien</a> <a href="/wiki/Pe%CC%8Dh-%C5%8De-j%C4%AB" title="Pe̍h-ōe-jī">POJ</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Min Nan Chinese-language romanization"><span lang="nan-Latn" style="font-style: normal">Tiong-bûn</span></span></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2" style="background-color: #dcffc9;"><a href="/wiki/Eastern_Min" title="Eastern Min">Eastern Min</a></th></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Fuzhou_dialect" title="Fuzhou dialect">Fuzhou</a> <a href="/wiki/Foochow_Romanized" title="Foochow Romanized">BUC</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Min Dong Chinese-language romanization"><span lang="cdo-Latn" style="font-style: normal">Dṳng-ùng</span></span></td></tr></tbody></table></td><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></tr><tr><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2" style="background-color: #b0c4de;">Han text (especially written and when distinguished from other <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">Simplified Chinese</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hans" style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Traditional Chinese</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant" style="font-size: 1rem;"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%BC%A2%E6%96%87" title="wikt:漢文">漢文</a></span></span></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;">Literal meaning</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han</a> <a href="/wiki/Text_(literary_theory)" title="Text (literary theory)">text</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Written_language" title="Written language">writing</a>)</td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="infobox-subbox collapsible collapsed" style="display:inline-table; text-align: left;"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" style="font-size: 100%; text-align: left; background-color: #f9ffbc;">Transcriptions</th></tr><tr><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2" style="background-color: #dcffc9;"><a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Mandarin</a></th></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hanyu_Pinyin" title="Hanyu Pinyin">Hanyu Pinyin</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Chinese-language romanization"><span lang="zh-Latn" style="font-style: normal">Hànwén</span></span></td></tr></tbody></table></td><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Chinese</b> (<a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hans">汉语</span>; <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">漢語</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Hànyǔ</span></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[b]</a></sup> or also <span lang="zh">中文</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Zhōngwén</span></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[c]</a></sup> especially for the written language) is a group of <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">languages</a> that form the <a href="/wiki/Sinitic_languages" title="Sinitic languages">Sinitic</a> branch of the <a href="/wiki/Sino-Tibetan_languages" title="Sino-Tibetan languages">Sino-Tibetan languages</a> family, spoken by the ethnic <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han Chinese</a> majority and <a href="/wiki/List_of_ethnic_groups_in_China" title="List of ethnic groups in China">many minority ethnic groups</a> in <a href="/wiki/Greater_China" title="Greater China">Greater China</a>. About 1.3 billion people (or approximately 16% of the world's population) speak a variety of Chinese as their <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">first language</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>The spoken <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese" title="Varieties of Chinese">varieties of Chinese</a> are usually considered by native speakers to be <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">variants of a single language</a>. However, their lack of <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">mutual intelligibility</a> means they are sometimes considered separate languages in a <a href="/wiki/Language_family" title="Language family">family</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[d]</a></sup> Investigation of the historical relationships among the varieties of Chinese is ongoing. Currently, most classifications posit 7 to 13 main regional groups based on phonetic developments from <a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">Middle Chinese</a>, of which the most spoken by far is <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_Chinese" title="Mandarin Chinese">Mandarin</a> (with about 800 million speakers, or 66%), followed by <a href="/wiki/Min_Chinese" title="Min Chinese">Min</a> (75 million, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Southern_Min" title="Southern Min">Southern Min</a>), <a href="/wiki/Wu_Chinese" title="Wu Chinese">Wu</a> (74 million, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Shanghainese" title="Shanghainese">Shanghainese</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Yue_Chinese" title="Yue Chinese">Yue</a> (68 million, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Cantonese" title="Cantonese">Cantonese</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChinese_Academy_of_Social_Sciences20123,_125_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChinese_Academy_of_Social_Sciences20123,_125-7">[3]</a></sup> These branches are unintelligible to each other, and many of their subgroups are unintelligible with the other varieties within the same branch (e.g. Southern Min). There are, however, transitional areas where varieties from different branches share enough features for some limited intelligibility, including <a href="/wiki/New_Xiang" title="New Xiang">New Xiang</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southwest_Mandarin" title="Southwest Mandarin">Southwest Mandarin</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xuanzhou_Wu" title="Xuanzhou Wu">Xuanzhou Wu</a> with <a href="/wiki/Lower_Yangtze_Mandarin" title="Lower Yangtze Mandarin">Lower Yangtze Mandarin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jin_Chinese" title="Jin Chinese">Jin</a> with <a href="/wiki/Central_Plains_Mandarin" title="Central Plains Mandarin">Central Plains Mandarin</a> and certain divergent dialects of <a href="/wiki/Hakka_Chinese" title="Hakka Chinese">Hakka</a> with <a href="/wiki/Gan_Chinese" title="Gan Chinese">Gan</a> (though these are unintelligible with mainstream Hakka). All varieties of Chinese are <a href="/wiki/Tone_(linguistics)" title="Tone (linguistics)">tonal</a> to at least some degree, and are largely <a href="/wiki/Analytic_language" title="Analytic language">analytic</a>.
</p><p>The earliest Chinese written records are <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a>-era <a href="/wiki/Oracle_bone_script" title="Oracle bone script">oracle bone inscriptions</a>, which can be dated to 1250 BCE. The phonetic categories of <a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese" title="Old Chinese">Old Chinese</a> can be reconstructed from the rhymes of ancient poetry. During the <a href="/wiki/Northern_and_Southern_dynasties" title="Northern and Southern dynasties">Northern and Southern dynasties</a> period, Middle Chinese went through several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sound_changes" title="Sound changes">sound changes</a> and split into several varieties following prolonged geographic and political separation. <i><a href="/wiki/Qieyun" title="Qieyun">Qieyun</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Rime_dictionary" title="Rime dictionary">rime dictionary</a>, recorded a compromise between the pronunciations of different regions. The royal courts of the Ming and early <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasties</a> operated using a <a href="/wiki/Koin%C3%A9_language" title="Koiné language">koiné language</a> (<a href="/wiki/Mandarin_(late_imperial_lingua_franca)" title="Mandarin (late imperial lingua franca)">Guanhua</a>) based on <a href="/wiki/Nanjing_dialect" title="Nanjing dialect">Nanjing dialect</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lower_Yangtze_Mandarin" title="Lower Yangtze Mandarin">Lower Yangtze Mandarin</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Chinese</a> (Standard Mandarin), based on the <a href="/wiki/Beijing_dialect" title="Beijing dialect">Beijing dialect</a> of Mandarin, was adopted in the 1930s and is now an official language of both the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China">People's Republic of China</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_China" title="Republic of China">Republic of China</a> (Taiwan), one of the four <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Singapore" title="Languages of Singapore">official languages of Singapore</a>, and one of the six <a href="/wiki/Official_languages_of_the_United_Nations" title="Official languages of the United Nations">official languages of the United Nations</a>. The written form, using the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Logograms" title="Logograms">logograms</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese characters</a>, is shared by literate speakers of mutually unintelligible dialects. Since the 1950s, <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese characters</a> have been promoted for use by the government of the People's Republic of China, while Singapore officially adopted simplified characters in 1976. <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Traditional characters</a> remain in use in Taiwan, Hong Kong, Macau, and other countries with significant overseas Chinese speaking communities such as Malaysia (which although adopted simplified characters as the <i>de facto</i> standard in the 1980s, traditional characters still remain in widespread use).
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_origin_and_spread_of_the_Sino-Tibetan_language_family.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1910" data-file-width="2851" decoding="async" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/The_origin_and_spread_of_the_Sino-Tibetan_language_family.png/400px-The_origin_and_spread_of_the_Sino-Tibetan_language_family.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/The_origin_and_spread_of_the_Sino-Tibetan_language_family.png/600px-The_origin_and_spread_of_the_Sino-Tibetan_language_family.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/The_origin_and_spread_of_the_Sino-Tibetan_language_family.png/800px-The_origin_and_spread_of_the_Sino-Tibetan_language_family.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_origin_and_spread_of_the_Sino-Tibetan_language_family.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>After applying the linguistic comparative method to the database of comparative linguistic data developed by <a href="/wiki/Laurent_Sagart" title="Laurent Sagart">Laurent Sagart</a> in 2019 to identify sound correspondences and establish cognates, phylogenetic methods are used to infer relationships among these languages and estimate the age of their origin and homeland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESagartJacquesLaiRyder201910319–10320_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESagartJacquesLaiRyder201910319–10320-8">[4]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Linguists classify all varieties of Chinese as part of the <a href="/wiki/Sino-Tibetan_languages" title="Sino-Tibetan languages">Sino-Tibetan language family</a>, together with <a href="/wiki/Burmese_language" title="Burmese language">Burmese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tibetic_languages" title="Tibetic languages">Tibetan</a> and many other languages spoken in the <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asian_Massif" title="Southeast Asian Massif">Southeast Asian Massif</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198812–13_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198812–13-9">[5]</a></sup> Although the relationship was first proposed in the early 19th century and is now broadly accepted, reconstruction of Sino-Tibetan is much less developed than that of families such as <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a> or <a href="/wiki/Austroasiatic_languages" title="Austroasiatic languages">Austroasiatic</a>. Difficulties have included the great diversity of the languages, the lack of <a href="/wiki/Inflection" title="Inflection">inflection</a> in many of them, and the effects of language contact. In addition, many of the smaller languages are spoken in mountainous areas that are difficult to reach and are often also sensitive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Border_zone" title="Border zone">border zones</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHandel2008422,_434–436_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHandel2008422,_434–436-10">[6]</a></sup> Without a secure reconstruction of proto-Sino-Tibetan, the higher-level structure of the family remains unclear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHandel2008426_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHandel2008426-11">[7]</a></sup> A top-level branching into Chinese and <a href="/wiki/Tibeto-Burman_languages" title="Tibeto-Burman languages">Tibeto-Burman languages</a> is often assumed, but has not been convincingly demonstrated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHandel2008431_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHandel2008431-12">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Chinese_language" title="History of the Chinese language">History of the Chinese language</a></div>
<p>The first written records appeared over 3,000 years ago during the <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a>. As the language evolved over this period, the various local varieties became mutually unintelligible. In reaction, central governments have repeatedly sought to promulgate a unified standard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988183–185_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988183–185-13">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_and_Middle_Chinese">Old and Middle Chinese</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Old and Middle Chinese">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The earliest examples of Chinese (<a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese" title="Old Chinese">Old Chinese</a>) are divinatory inscriptions on <a href="/wiki/Oracle_bone" title="Oracle bone">oracle bones</a> from around 1250 BCE in the late <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchuessler20071_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchuessler20071-14">[10]</a></sup> The next attested stage came from  <a href="/wiki/Chinese_bronze_inscriptions" title="Chinese bronze inscriptions">inscriptions on bronze artifacts</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Western_Zhou" title="Western Zhou">Western Zhou</a> period (1046–771 BCE), the <i><a href="/wiki/Classic_of_Poetry" title="Classic of Poetry">Classic of Poetry</a></i> and portions of the <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Documents" title="Book of Documents">Book of Documents</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/I_Ching" title="I Ching">I Ching</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter19922–3_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter19922–3-15">[11]</a></sup> Scholars have attempted to reconstruct the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phonology_of_Old_Chinese" title="Phonology of Old Chinese">phonology of Old Chinese</a> by comparing later varieties of Chinese with the rhyming practice of the <i>Classic of Poetry</i> and the phonetic elements found in the majority of Chinese characters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198842–45_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198842–45-16">[12]</a></sup> Although many of the finer details remain unclear, most scholars agree that Old Chinese differs from Middle Chinese in lacking retroflex and palatal obstruents but having initial <a href="/wiki/Consonant_cluster" title="Consonant cluster">consonant clusters</a> of some sort, and in having voiceless nasals and liquids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992177_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992177-17">[13]</a></sup> Most recent reconstructions also describe an atonal language with consonant clusters at the end of the syllable, developing into <a href="/wiki/Tone_(linguistics)" title="Tone (linguistics)">tone</a> distinctions in Middle Chinese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992181–183_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992181–183-18">[14]</a></sup>  Several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Derivational_affix" title="Derivational affix">derivational affixes</a> have also been identified, but the language lacks <a href="/wiki/Inflection" title="Inflection">inflection</a>, and indicated grammatical relationships using word order and <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_particle" title="Grammatical particle">grammatical particles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchuessler200712_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchuessler200712-19">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">Middle Chinese</a> was the language used during <a href="/wiki/Northern_and_Southern_dynasties" title="Northern and Southern dynasties">Northern and Southern dynasties</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sui_dynasty" title="Sui dynasty">Sui</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song</a> dynasties (6th through 10th centuries CE). It can be divided into an early period, reflected by the <i><a href="/wiki/Qieyun" title="Qieyun">Qieyun</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Rime_dictionary" title="Rime dictionary">rime book</a> (601 CE), and a late period in the 10th century, reflected by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhyme_table" title="Rhyme table">rhyme tables</a> such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Yunjing" title="Yunjing">Yunjing</a></i> constructed by ancient Chinese philologists as a guide to the <i>Qieyun</i> system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter199214–15_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter199214–15-20">[16]</a></sup> These works define phonological categories, but with little hint of what sounds they represent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERamsey1987125_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERamsey1987125-21">[17]</a></sup> Linguists have identified these sounds by comparing the categories with pronunciations in modern <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese" title="Varieties of Chinese">varieties of Chinese</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sino-Xenic_vocabularies" title="Sino-Xenic vocabularies">borrowed Chinese words</a> in Japanese, Vietnamese, and Korean, and transcription evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198834–42_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198834–42-22">[18]</a></sup> The resulting system is very complex, with a large number of consonants and vowels, but they are probably not all distinguished in any single dialect. Most linguists now believe it represents a <a href="/wiki/Diasystem" title="Diasystem">diasystem</a> encompassing 6th-century northern and southern standards for reading the classics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198824_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198824-23">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_and_literary_forms">Classical and literary forms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Classical and literary forms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Classical_Chinese" title="Classical Chinese">Classical Chinese</a></div>
<p>The relationship between spoken and written Chinese is rather complex ("<a href="/wiki/Diglossia" title="Diglossia">diglossia</a>"). Its spoken varieties have evolved at different rates, while written Chinese itself has changed much less. <a href="/wiki/Classical_Chinese" title="Classical Chinese">Classical Chinese</a> <a href="/wiki/Literature" title="Literature">literature</a> began in the <a href="/wiki/Spring_and_Autumn_period" title="Spring and Autumn period">Spring and Autumn period</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rise_of_northern_dialects">Rise of northern dialects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Rise of northern dialects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the fall of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Song" title="Northern Song">Northern Song</a> dynasty and subsequent reign of the <a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(1115%E2%80%931234)" title="Jin dynasty (1115–1234)">Jin</a> (Jurchen) and <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan</a> (Mongol) dynasties in northern China, a common speech (now called <a href="/wiki/Old_Mandarin" title="Old Mandarin">Old Mandarin</a>) developed based on the dialects of the <a href="/wiki/North_China_Plain" title="North China Plain">North China Plain</a> around the capital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198848_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198848-24">[20]</a></sup>
The <i><a href="/wiki/Zhongyuan_Yinyun" title="Zhongyuan Yinyun">Zhongyuan Yinyun</a></i> (1324) was a dictionary that codified the rhyming conventions of new <i><a href="/wiki/Sanqu" title="Sanqu">sanqu</a></i> verse form in this language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198848–49_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198848–49-25">[21]</a></sup>
Together with the slightly later <i><a href="/wiki/Menggu_Ziyun" title="Menggu Ziyun">Menggu Ziyun</a></i>, this dictionary describes a language with many of the features characteristic of modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mandarin_dialects" title="Mandarin dialects">Mandarin dialects</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198849–51_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198849–51-26">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Up to the early 20th century, most Chinese people only spoke their local variety.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988133,_247_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988133,_247-27">[23]</a></sup>
Thus, as a practical measure, officials of the <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming</a> and <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing</a> dynasties carried out the administration of the empire using a <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_(late_imperial_lingua_franca)" title="Mandarin (late imperial lingua franca)">common language based on Mandarin varieties</a>, known as <i>Guānhuà</i> (<span lang="zh-Hans">官话</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">官話</span>, literally "language of officials").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988136_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988136-28">[24]</a></sup>
For most of this period, this language was a <a href="/wiki/Koin%C3%A9_language" title="Koiné language">koiné</a> based on dialects spoken in the <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a> area, though not identical to any single dialect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoblin2000549–550_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoblin2000549–550-29">[25]</a></sup>
By the middle of the 19th century, the Beijing dialect had become dominant and was essential for any business with the imperial court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoblin2000540–541_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoblin2000540–541-30">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1930s, a <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">standard national language</a>, <i>Guóyǔ</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hans">国语</span></span>/<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">國語</span></span> ; "national language") was adopted. After much dispute between proponents of northern and southern dialects and an abortive attempt at an artificial pronunciation, the <a href="/wiki/National_Languages_Committee" title="National Languages Committee">National Language Unification Commission</a> finally settled on the Beijing dialect in 1932. The People's Republic founded in 1949 retained this standard but renamed it <i>pǔtōnghuà</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hans">普通话</span></span>/<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hans">普通話</span></span>; "common speech").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERamsey19873–15_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERamsey19873–15-31">[27]</a></sup> The national language is now used in education, the media, and formal situations in both Mainland China and Taiwan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988133_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988133-32">[28]</a></sup> Because of their colonial and linguistic history, the language used in education, the media, formal speech, and everyday life in <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a> and <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">Macau</a> is the local <a href="/wiki/Cantonese" title="Cantonese">Cantonese</a>, although the standard language, Mandarin, has become very influential and is being taught in schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZhangYang2004_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZhangYang2004-33">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence">Influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Adoption_of_Chinese_literary_culture" title="Adoption of Chinese literary culture">Adoption of Chinese literary culture</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sino-Xenic_vocabularies" title="Sino-Xenic vocabularies">Sino-Xenic vocabularies</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tripitaka_Koreana.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1163" data-file-width="1357" decoding="async" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Tripitaka_Koreana.jpg/220px-Tripitaka_Koreana.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Tripitaka_Koreana.jpg/330px-Tripitaka_Koreana.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Tripitaka_Koreana.jpg/440px-Tripitaka_Koreana.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tripitaka_Koreana.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i><a href="/wiki/Tripitaka_Koreana" title="Tripitaka Koreana">Tripitaka Koreana</a></i>, a Korean collection of the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Buddhist_canon" title="Chinese Buddhist canon">Chinese Buddhist canon</a></div></div></div>
<p>Historically, the Chinese language has spread to its neighbors through a variety of means. Northern Vietnam was incorporated into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_empire" title="Han empire">Han empire</a> in 111 BCE, marking the beginning of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_domination_of_Vietnam" title="Chinese domination of Vietnam">period of Chinese control</a> that ran almost continuously for a millennium. The <a href="/wiki/Four_Commanderies_of_Han" title="Four Commanderies of Han">Four Commanderies</a> were established in northern Korea in the first century BCE, but disintegrated in the following centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESohnLee200323_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESohnLee200323-34">[30]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Buddhism" title="Chinese Buddhism">Chinese Buddhism</a> spread over East Asia between the 2nd and 5th centuries CE, and with it the study of scriptures and literature in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Literary_Chinese" title="Literary Chinese">Literary Chinese</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller196729–30_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller196729–30-35">[31]</a></sup> Later <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> developed strong central governments modeled on Chinese institutions, with Literary Chinese as the language of administration and scholarship, a position it would retain until the late 19th century in <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a> and (to a lesser extent) Japan, and the early 20th century in Vietnam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKornicki201175–77_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKornicki201175–77-36">[32]</a></sup> Scholars from different lands could communicate, albeit only in writing, using Literary Chinese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKornicki201167_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKornicki201167-37">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although they used Chinese solely for written communication, each country had its own tradition of reading texts aloud, the so-called <a href="/wiki/Sino-Xenic_pronunciations" title="Sino-Xenic pronunciations">Sino-Xenic pronunciations</a>. Chinese words with these pronunciations were also extensively imported into the <a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_language" title="Vietnamese language">Vietnamese</a> languages, and today comprise over half of their vocabularies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiyake200498–99_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiyake200498–99-38">[34]</a></sup> This massive influx led to changes in the phonological structure of the languages, contributing to the development of <a href="/wiki/Mora_(linguistics)" title="Mora (linguistics)">moraic</a> structure in Japanese<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990120–121_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990120–121-39">[35]</a></sup> and the disruption of <a href="/wiki/Vowel_harmony" title="Vowel harmony">vowel harmony</a> in Korean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESohn200189_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESohn200189-40">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Borrowed Chinese morphemes have been used extensively in all these languages to coin compound words for new concepts, in a similar way to the use of <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> roots in European languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990146_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990146-41">[37]</a></sup> Many new compounds, or new meanings for old phrases, were created in the late 19th and early 20th centuries to name Western concepts and artifacts. These coinages, written in shared Chinese characters, have then been borrowed freely between languages. They have even been accepted into Chinese, a language usually resistant to loanwords, because their foreign origin was hidden by their written form. Often different compounds for the same concept were in circulation for some time before a winner emerged, and sometimes the final choice differed between countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200043_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200043-42">[38]</a></sup> The proportion of vocabulary of Chinese origin thus tends to be greater in technical, abstract, or formal language. For example, in Japan, <a href="/wiki/Sino-Japanese_vocabulary" title="Sino-Japanese vocabulary">Sino-Japanese words</a> account for about 35% of the words in entertainment magazines, over half the words in newspapers, and 60% of the words in science magazines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990143_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990143-43">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>Vietnam, Korea, and Japan each developed writing systems for their own languages, initially based on <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese characters</a>, but later replaced with the <i><a href="/wiki/Hangul" title="Hangul">hangul</a></i> alphabet for Korean and supplemented with <i><a href="/wiki/Kana" title="Kana">kana</a></i> syllabaries for Japanese, while Vietnamese continued to be written with the complex <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ch%E1%BB%AF_n%C3%B4m" title="Chữ nôm">chữ nôm</a></i> script. However, these were limited to popular literature until the late 19th century. Today Japanese is written with a composite script using both Chinese characters (<i><a href="/wiki/Kanji" title="Kanji">kanji</a></i>) and kana. Korean is written exclusively with hangul in North Korea (although knowledge of the supplementary Chinese characters - <i><a href="/wiki/Hanja" title="Hanja">hanja</a></i> - is still required), and hanja are increasingly rarely used in South Korea. As a result of former <a href="/wiki/French_Indochina" title="French Indochina">French colonization</a>, Vietnamese switched to <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_alphabet" title="Vietnamese alphabet">a Latin-based alphabet</a>.
</p><p>Examples of <a href="/wiki/List_of_English_words_of_Chinese_origin" title="List of English words of Chinese origin">loan words in English</a> include "<a href="/wiki/Tea" title="Tea">tea</a>", from <a href="/wiki/Hokkien" title="Hokkien">Hokkien</a> (Min Nan) <i><span lang="nan-Latn"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/t%C3%AA" title="wikt:tê">tê</a></span></i> (<span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%8C%B6#Chinese" title="wikt:茶">茶</a></span>), "<a href="/wiki/Dim_sum" title="Dim sum">dim sum</a>", from Cantonese <i>dim<sup>2</sup> sam<sup>1</sup></i> (<span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%BB%9E%E5%BF%83#Chinese" title="wikt:點心">點心</a></span>) and "<a href="/wiki/Kumquat" title="Kumquat">kumquat</a>", from Cantonese <i>gam<sup>1</sup>gwat<sup>1</sup></i> (<span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%87%91%E6%A9%98#Chinese" title="wikt:金橘">金橘</a></span>).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Varieties">Varieties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Varieties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese" title="Varieties of Chinese">Varieties of Chinese</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_sinitic_languages_full-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1759" data-file-width="2219" decoding="async" height="349" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Map_of_sinitic_languages_full-en.svg/440px-Map_of_sinitic_languages_full-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Map_of_sinitic_languages_full-en.svg/660px-Map_of_sinitic_languages_full-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Map_of_sinitic_languages_full-en.svg/880px-Map_of_sinitic_languages_full-en.svg.png 2x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_sinitic_languages_full-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Range of Chinese dialect groups in China Mainland and Taiwan according to the <i>Language Atlas of China</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWurmLiBaumannLee1987_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWurmLiBaumannLee1987-44">[40]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jerry_Norman_(sinologist)" title="Jerry Norman (sinologist)">Jerry Norman</a> estimated that there are hundreds of mutually unintelligible varieties of Chinese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman200372_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman200372-45">[41]</a></sup> These varieties form a <a href="/wiki/Dialect_continuum" title="Dialect continuum">dialect continuum</a>, in which differences in speech generally become more pronounced as distances increase, though the rate of change varies immensely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988189–190_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988189–190-46">[42]</a></sup> Generally, mountainous South China exhibits more linguistic diversity than the <a href="/wiki/North_China_Plain" title="North China Plain">North China Plain</a>. In parts of South China, a major city's dialect may only be marginally intelligible to close neighbors. For instance, <a href="/wiki/Wuzhou" title="Wuzhou">Wuzhou</a> is about 190 kilometres (120 mi) upstream from <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a>, but the <a href="/wiki/Yue_Chinese" title="Yue Chinese">Yue</a> variety spoken there is more like that of Guangzhou than is that of <a href="/wiki/Taishan,_Guangdong" title="Taishan, Guangdong">Taishan</a>, 95 kilometres (60 mi) southwest of Guangzhou and separated from it by several rivers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERamsey198723_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERamsey198723-47">[43]</a></sup> In parts of <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a> the speech of neighboring counties or even villages may be mutually unintelligible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988188_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988188-48">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Until the late 20th century, Chinese emigrants to Southeast Asia and North America came from southeast coastal areas, where Min, Hakka, and Yue dialects are spoken.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988191_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988191-49">[45]</a></sup>
The vast majority of Chinese immigrants to North America up to the mid-20th century spoke the <a href="/wiki/Taishanese" title="Taishanese">Taishan dialect</a>, from a small coastal area southwest of <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERamsey198798_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERamsey198798-50">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Grouping">Grouping</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Grouping">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="PieChartTemplate thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px">
<div class="mw-no-invert" style="background-color:white;margin:auto;position:relative;width:200px;height:200px;overflow:hidden;border-radius:100px;border:1px solid black;transform:scaleX(-1)rotate(-90deg)">

<div class="thumbcaption">
<p>Proportions of first-language speakers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChinese_Academy_of_Social_Sciences20123,_125_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChinese_Academy_of_Social_Sciences20123,_125-7">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ac8761; color:black;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_Chinese" title="Mandarin Chinese">Mandarin</a> (65.7%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#b1bab6; color:black;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Min_Chinese" title="Min Chinese">Min</a> (6.2%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#c8c8a8; color:black;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Wu_Chinese" title="Wu Chinese">Wu</a> (6.1%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#bf6e7b; color:black;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Yue_Chinese" title="Yue Chinese">Yue</a> (5.6%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#96642c; color:white;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Jin_Chinese" title="Jin Chinese">Jin</a> (5.2%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#fbd98d; color:black;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Gan_Chinese" title="Gan Chinese">Gan</a> (3.9%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#eeb36a; color:black;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Hakka_Chinese" title="Hakka Chinese">Hakka</a> (3.5%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#a5ae87; color:black;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Xiang_Chinese" title="Xiang Chinese">Xiang</a> (3.0%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#9b987f; color:black;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Huizhou_Chinese" title="Huizhou Chinese">Huizhou</a> (0.3%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#946565; color:white;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Pinghua" title="Pinghua">Pinghua</a>, others (0.6%)</div>
</div>
</div></div>
<p>Local varieties of Chinese are conventionally classified into seven dialect groups, largely on the basis of the different evolution of <a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">Middle Chinese</a> voiced initials:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988181_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988181-51">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201053–55_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201053–55-52">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Mandarin_Chinese" title="Mandarin Chinese">Mandarin</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Chinese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beijing_dialect" title="Beijing dialect">Pekingese</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sichuanese_Mandarin" title="Sichuanese Mandarin">Sichuanese</a>, and also the <a href="/wiki/Dungan_language" title="Dungan language">Dungan language</a> spoken in <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wu_Chinese" title="Wu Chinese">Wu</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Shanghainese" title="Shanghainese">Shanghainese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Suzhou_dialect" title="Suzhou dialect">Suzhounese</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wenzhounese" title="Wenzhounese">Wenzhounese</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gan_Chinese" title="Gan Chinese">Gan</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Xiang_Chinese" title="Xiang Chinese">Xiang</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Min_Chinese" title="Min Chinese">Min</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Fuzhou_dialect" title="Fuzhou dialect">Fuzhounese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hainanese" title="Hainanese">Hainanese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hokkien" title="Hokkien">Hokkien</a> and <a href="/wiki/Teochew_dialect" title="Teochew dialect">Teochew</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hakka_Chinese" title="Hakka Chinese">Hakka</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Yue_Chinese" title="Yue Chinese">Yue</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Cantonese" title="Cantonese">Cantonese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taishanese" title="Taishanese">Taishanese</a></li></ul>
<p>The classification of <a href="/wiki/Li_Rong_(linguist)" title="Li Rong (linguist)">Li Rong</a>, which is used in the <i><a href="/wiki/Language_Atlas_of_China" title="Language Atlas of China">Language Atlas of China</a></i> (1987), distinguishes three further groups:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWurmLiBaumannLee1987_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWurmLiBaumannLee1987-44">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201055–56_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201055–56-53">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Jin_Chinese" title="Jin Chinese">Jin</a>, previously included in Mandarin.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Huizhou_Chinese" title="Huizhou Chinese">Huizhou</a>, previously included in Wu.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pinghua_Chinese" title="Pinghua Chinese">Pinghua</a>, previously included in Yue.</li></ul>
<p>Some varieties remain unclassified, including <a href="/wiki/Danzhou_dialect" title="Danzhou dialect">Danzhou dialect</a> (spoken in <a href="/wiki/Danzhou" title="Danzhou">Danzhou</a>, on <a href="/wiki/Hainan" title="Hainan">Hainan</a> Island), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waxianghua" title="Waxianghua">Waxianghua</a> (spoken in western <a href="/wiki/Hunan" title="Hunan">Hunan</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Shaozhou_Tuhua" title="Shaozhou Tuhua">Shaozhou Tuhua</a> (spoken in northern <a href="/wiki/Guangdong" title="Guangdong">Guangdong</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201072–73_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201072–73-54">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Standard_Chinese">Standard Chinese</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Standard Chinese">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Chinese</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_territories_where_Chinese_is_an_official_language" title="List of countries and territories where Chinese is an official language">List of countries and territories where Chinese is an official language</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Chinese</a>, often called Mandarin, is the official <a href="/wiki/Standard_language" title="Standard language">standard language</a> of <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, de facto official language of <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>, and one of the four official languages of <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> (where it is called "Huáyŭ" <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hans">华语</span></span>/<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">華語</span></span> or simply Chinese). Standard Chinese is based on the <a href="/wiki/Beijing_dialect" title="Beijing dialect">Beijing dialect</a>, the dialect of <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_Chinese" title="Mandarin Chinese">Mandarin</a> as spoken in <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>. The governments of both China and Taiwan intend for speakers of all Chinese speech varieties to use it as a common language of communication. Therefore, it is used in government agencies, in the media, and as a language of instruction in schools.
</p><p>In China and Taiwan, <a href="/wiki/Diglossia" title="Diglossia">diglossia</a> has been a common feature. For example, in addition to Standard Chinese, a resident of <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a> might speak <a href="/wiki/Shanghainese" title="Shanghainese">Shanghainese</a>; and, if they grew up elsewhere, then they are also likely to be fluent in the particular dialect of that local area. A native of <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a> may speak both Cantonese and Standard Chinese. In addition to Mandarin, most <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_people" title="Taiwanese people">Taiwanese</a> also speak <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_Hokkien" title="Taiwanese Hokkien">Taiwanese Hokkien</a> (commonly "Taiwanese" <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">台語</span></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[52]</a></sup>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hakka_language" title="Hakka language">Hakka</a>, or an <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_languages" title="Austronesian languages">Austronesian language</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[53]</a></sup> A Taiwanese may commonly mix pronunciations, phrases, and words from Mandarin and other <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Taiwan" title="Languages of Taiwan">Taiwanese languages</a>, and this mixture is considered normal in daily or informal speech.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to their traditional cultural ties to <a href="/wiki/Guangdong" title="Guangdong">Guangdong</a> province and colonial histories, <a href="/wiki/Cantonese" title="Cantonese">Cantonese</a> is used as the standard variant of Chinese in <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a> and <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">Macau</a> instead.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nomenclature">Nomenclature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Nomenclature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The official Chinese designation for the major branches of Chinese is <i>fāngyán</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">方言</span></span>, literally "regional speech"), whereas the more closely related varieties within these are called <i>dìdiǎn fāngyán</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hans">地点方言</span></span>/<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">地點方言</span></span> "local speech").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeFrancis198457_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeFrancis198457-59">[55]</a></sup> Conventional English-language usage in Chinese linguistics is to use <i>dialect</i> for the speech of a particular place (regardless of status) and <i>dialect group</i> for a regional grouping such as Mandarin or Wu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman200372_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman200372-45">[41]</a></sup> Because varieties from different groups are not <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mutually_intelligible" title="Mutually intelligible">mutually intelligible</a>, some scholars prefer to describe Wu and others as separate languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomason198827–28_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomason198827–28-60">[56]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs a source specifically saying that scholars do this and for this reason (June 2015)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Norman_(sinologist)" title="Jerry Norman (sinologist)">Jerry Norman</a> called this practice misleading, pointing out that Wu, which itself contains many mutually unintelligible varieties, could not be properly called a single language under the same criterion, and that the same is true for each of the other groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman200372_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman200372-45">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mutual intelligibility is considered by some linguists to be the main criterion for determining whether varieties are separate languages or dialects of a single language,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMair199117_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMair199117-61">[57]</a></sup> although others do not regard it as decisive,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeFrancis198454_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeFrancis198454-62">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERomaine200013,_23_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERomaine200013,_23-63">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWardaughFuller201428–32_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWardaughFuller201428–32-64">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiang201411–14_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiang201411–14-65">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHymes197164_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHymes197164-66">[62]</a></sup> particularly when cultural factors interfere as they do with Chinese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomason198827_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomason198827-67">[63]</a></sup> As <a href="#CITEREFCampbell2008">Campbell (2008)</a> explains, linguists often ignore mutual intelligibility when varieties share intelligibility with a central variety (i.e. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prestige_variety" title="Prestige variety">prestige variety</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_Mandarin" title="Standard Mandarin">Standard Mandarin</a>), as the issue requires some careful handling when mutual intelligibility is inconsistent with language identity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell2008637_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell2008637-68">[64]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_DeFrancis" title="John DeFrancis">John DeFrancis</a> argues that it is inappropriate to refer to Mandarin, Wu and so on as "dialects" because the mutual unintelligibility between them is too great. On the other hand, he also objects to considering them as separate languages, as it incorrectly implies a set of disruptive "religious, economic, political, and other differences" between speakers that exist, for example, between <a href="/wiki/French_language_in_Canada" title="French language in Canada">French Catholics</a> and English Protestants in Canada, but not between speakers of Cantonese and Mandarin in China, owing to China's near-uninterrupted history of centralized government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeFrancis198455–57_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeFrancis198455–57-69">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of the difficulties involved in determining the difference between language and dialect, other terms have been proposed. These include <i>vernacular</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaugen1966927_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaugen1966927-70">[66]</a></sup> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lect" title="Lect">lect</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[67]</a></sup> <i>regionalect</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeFrancis198457_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeFrancis198457-59">[55]</a></sup> <i>topolect</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMair19917_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMair19917-72">[68]</a></sup> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Language_variety" title="Language variety">variety</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHudson199622_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHudson199622-73">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most Chinese people consider the spoken varieties as one single language because speakers share a common culture and history, as well as a shared national identity and a common written form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter19927–8_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter19927–8-74">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Phonology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese_phonology" title="Standard Chinese phonology">Standard Chinese phonology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Historical_Chinese_phonology" title="Historical Chinese phonology">Historical Chinese phonology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese#Phonology" title="Varieties of Chinese">Varieties of Chinese § Phonology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><audio class="thumbimage" controls="" data-durationhint="30" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Edmund_Yeo_-_voice_-_ch_150127_1828.wav" id="mwe_player_0" preload="none" style="width:220px;" width="220"><source data-bandwidth="95072" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg Vorbis" data-title="Ogg Vorbis" data-transcodekey="ogg" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/27/Edmund_Yeo_-_voice_-_ch_150127_1828.wav/Edmund_Yeo_-_voice_-_ch_150127_1828.wav.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="170328" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/27/Edmund_Yeo_-_voice_-_ch_150127_1828.wav/Edmund_Yeo_-_voice_-_ch_150127_1828.wav.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/><source data-bandwidth="768000" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="WAV source" data-title="Original WAV file (768 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/27/Edmund_Yeo_-_voice_-_ch_150127_1828.wav" type="audio/wav"/></audio> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Edmund_Yeo_-_voice_-_ch_150127_1828.wav" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Malaysian man speaking <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_Chinese" title="Mandarin Chinese">Mandarin</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Malaysian_Mandarin" title="Malaysian Mandarin">Malaysian</a> accent</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">phonological</a> structure of each syllable consists of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syllable_nucleus" title="Syllable nucleus">nucleus</a> that has a <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">vowel</a> (which can be a <a href="/wiki/Monophthong" title="Monophthong">monophthong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">diphthong</a>, or even a <a href="/wiki/Triphthong" title="Triphthong">triphthong</a> in certain varieties), preceded by an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syllable_onset" title="Syllable onset">onset</a> (a single <a href="/wiki/Consonant" title="Consonant">consonant</a>, or consonant+<a href="/wiki/Semivowel" title="Semivowel">glide</a>; zero onset is also possible), and followed (optionally) by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syllable_coda" title="Syllable coda">coda</a> consonant; a syllable also carries a <a href="/wiki/Tone_(linguistics)" title="Tone (linguistics)">tone</a>. There are some instances where a vowel is not used as a nucleus. An example of this is in <a href="/wiki/Cantonese" title="Cantonese">Cantonese</a>, where the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nasal_stop" title="Nasal stop">nasal</a> <a href="/wiki/Sonorant" title="Sonorant">sonorant</a> consonants <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/m/</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ŋ/</span> can stand alone as their own syllable.
</p><p>In Mandarin much more than in other spoken varieties, most syllables tend to be open syllables, meaning they have no coda (assuming that a final <a href="/wiki/Semivowel" title="Semivowel">glide</a> is not analyzed as a coda), but syllables that do have codas are restricted to nasals <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/m/</span>, <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/n/</span>, <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ŋ/</span>, the retroflex approximant <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɻ /</span>, and voiceless stops <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/p/</span>, <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/t/</span>, <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/k/</span>, or <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ʔ/</span>. Some varieties allow most of these codas, whereas others, such as <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Chinese</a>, are limited to only <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/n/</span>, <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ŋ/</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɻ/</span>.
</p><p>The number of sounds in the different spoken dialects varies, but in general there has been a tendency to a reduction in sounds from <a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">Middle Chinese</a>. The Mandarin dialects in particular have experienced a dramatic decrease in sounds and so have far more multisyllabic words than most other spoken varieties. The total number of syllables in some varieties is therefore only about a thousand, including tonal variation, which is only about an eighth as many as English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeFrancis_p42_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-DeFrancis_p42-75">[e]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tones">Tones</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Tones">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>All varieties of spoken Chinese use <a href="/wiki/Tone_(linguistics)" title="Tone (linguistics)">tones</a> to distinguish words.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198852_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198852-76">[71]</a></sup> A few dialects of north China may have as few as three tones, while some dialects in south China have up to 6 or 12 tones, depending on how one counts. One exception from this is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shanghainese_dialect" title="Shanghainese dialect">Shanghainese</a> which has reduced the set of tones to a two-toned <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pitch_accent" title="Pitch accent">pitch accent</a> system much like modern Japanese.
</p><p>A very common example used to illustrate the use of tones in Chinese is the application of the four tones of <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Chinese</a> (along with the neutral tone) to the syllable <i>ma</i>. The tones are exemplified by the following five Chinese words:
</p>

<p>Standard <a href="/wiki/Cantonese" title="Cantonese">Cantonese</a>, in contrast, has six tones. Historically, finals that end in a <a href="/wiki/Plosive" title="Plosive">stop consonant</a> were considered to be "<a href="/wiki/Checked_tone" title="Checked tone">checked tones</a>" and thus counted separately for a total of nine tones. However, they are considered to be duplicates in modern linguistics and are no longer counted as such:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatthewsYip199420–22_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthewsYip199420–22-77">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
