<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">Grammar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Grammar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_grammar" title="Chinese grammar">Chinese grammar</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_classifiers" title="Chinese classifiers">Chinese classifiers</a></div>
<p>Chinese is often described as a "monosyllabic" language. However, this is only partially correct. It is largely accurate when describing <a href="/wiki/Classical_Chinese" title="Classical Chinese">Classical Chinese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">Middle Chinese</a>; in Classical Chinese, for example, perhaps 90% of words correspond to a single syllable and a single character. In the modern varieties, it is usually the case that a <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">morpheme</a> (unit of meaning) is a single syllable; in contrast, English has many multi-syllable morphemes, both bound and free, such as "seven", "elephant", "para-" and "-able".
</p><p>Some of the conservative southern varieties of modern Chinese have largely monosyllabic words, especially among the more basic vocabulary. In modern Mandarin, however, most <a href="/wiki/Noun" title="Noun">nouns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adjective" title="Adjective">adjectives</a> and <a href="/wiki/Verb" title="Verb">verbs</a> are largely disyllabic. A significant cause of this is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phonetic_erosion" title="Phonetic erosion">phonological attrition</a>. <a href="/wiki/Sound_change" title="Sound change">Sound change</a> over time has steadily reduced the number of possible syllables. In modern Mandarin, there are now only about 1,200 possible syllables, including tonal distinctions, compared with about 5,000 in <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_language" title="Vietnamese language">Vietnamese</a> (still largely monosyllabic) and over 8,000 in English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeFrancis_p42_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-DeFrancis_p42-75">[e]</a></sup>
</p><p>This phonological collapse has led to a corresponding increase in the number of <a href="/wiki/Homophone" title="Homophone">homophones</a>. As an example, the small Langenscheidt Pocket Chinese Dictionary<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[73]</a></sup> lists six words that are commonly pronounced as <i>shí</i> (tone 2): <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">十</span></span> 'ten'; <span lang="zh-Hans">实</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">實</span> 'real, actual'; <span lang="zh-Hans">识</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">識</span> 'know (a person), recognize'; <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">石</span></span> 'stone'; <span lang="zh-Hans">时</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">時</span> 'time'; <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">食</span></span> 'food, eat'. These were all pronounced differently in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Chinese" title="Early Middle Chinese">Early Middle Chinese</a>; in <a href="/wiki/Baxter%27s_transcription_for_Middle_Chinese" title="Baxter's transcription for Middle Chinese">William H. Baxter's transcription</a> they were <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">dzyip</i></span>, <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">zyit</i></span>, <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">syik</i></span>, <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">dzyek</i></span>, <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">dzyi</i></span> and <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">zyik</i></span> respectively. They are still pronounced differently in today's <a href="/wiki/Cantonese" title="Cantonese">Cantonese</a>; in <a href="/wiki/Jyutping" title="Jyutping">Jyutping</a> they are <i>sap9</i>, <i>sat9</i>, <i>sik7</i>, <i>sek9</i>, <i>si4</i>, <i>sik9</i>. In modern spoken Mandarin, however, tremendous ambiguity would result if all of these words could be used as-is; <a href="/wiki/Yuen_Ren_Chao" title="Yuen Ren Chao">Yuen Ren Chao</a>'s modern poem <a href="/wiki/Lion-Eating_Poet_in_the_Stone_Den" title="Lion-Eating Poet in the Stone Den">Lion-Eating Poet in the Stone Den</a> exploits this, consisting of 92 characters all pronounced <i>shi</i>. As such, most of these words have been replaced (in speech, if not in writing) with a longer, less-ambiguous compound. Only the first one, <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">十</span></span> 'ten', normally appears as such when spoken; the rest are normally replaced with, respectively, <i>shíjì</i> <span lang="zh-Hans">实际</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">實際</span> (lit. 'actual-connection'); <i>rènshi</i> <span lang="zh-Hans">认识</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">認識</span> (lit. 'recognize-know'); <i>shítou</i> <span lang="zh-Hans">石头</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">石頭</span> (lit. 'stone-head'); <i>shíjiān</i> <span lang="zh-Hans">时间</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">時間</span> (lit. 'time-interval'); <i>shíwù</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">食物</span></span> (lit. 'foodstuff'). In each case, the homophone was disambiguated by adding another morpheme, typically either a synonym or a generic word of some sort (for example, 'head', 'thing'), the purpose of which is simply to indicate which of the possible meanings of the other, homophonic syllable should be selected.
</p><p>However, when one of the above words forms part of a compound, the disambiguating syllable is generally dropped and the resulting word is still disyllabic. For example, <i>shí</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">石</span></span> alone, not <i>shítou</i> <span lang="zh-Hans">石头</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">石頭</span>, appears in compounds meaning 'stone-', for example, <i>shígāo</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">石膏</span></span> 'plaster' (lit. 'stone cream'), <i>shíhuī</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">石灰</span></span> 'lime' (lit. 'stone dust'), <i>shíkū</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">石窟</span></span> 'grotto' (lit. 'stone cave'), <i>shíyīng</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">石英</span></span> 'quartz' (lit. 'stone flower'), <i>shíyóu</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">石油</span></span> 'petroleum' (lit. 'stone oil').
</p><p>Most modern varieties of Chinese have the tendency to form new words through disyllabic, trisyllabic and tetra-character <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compound_word" title="Compound word">compounds</a>. In some cases, monosyllabic words have become disyllabic without compounding, as in <i>kūlong</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">窟窿</span></span> from <i>kǒng</i> 孔; this is especially common in <a href="/wiki/Jin_Chinese" title="Jin Chinese">Jin</a>.
</p><p>Chinese <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">morphology</a> is strictly bound to a set number of <a href="/wiki/Syllable" title="Syllable">syllables</a> with a fairly rigid construction. Although many of these single-syllable morphemes (<i>zì</i>, <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">字</span></span>) can stand alone as individual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Word_(linguistics)" title="Word (linguistics)">words</a>, they more often than not form multi-syllabic <a href="/wiki/Compound_(linguistics)" title="Compound (linguistics)">compounds</a>, known as <i>cí</i> (<span lang="zh-Hans">词</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">詞</span>), which more closely resembles the traditional Western notion of a word. A Chinese <i>cí</i> ('word') can consist of more than one character-morpheme, usually two, but there can be three or more.
</p><p>For example:
</p>
<ul><li><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn">yún</i></span> <span lang="zh-Hans"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E4%BA%91#Simplified_Chinese" title="wikt:云">云</a></span>/<span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%9B%B2#Traditional_Chinese" title="wikt:雲">雲</a></span> 'cloud'</li>
<li><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn">hànbǎobāo</i></span>, <span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn">hànbǎo</i></span> <span lang="zh-Hans"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%B1%89%E5%A0%A1%E5%8C%85#Simplified_Chinese" title="wikt:汉堡包">汉堡包</a></span>/<span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%BC%A2%E5%A0%A1%E5%8C%85#Traditional_Chinese" title="wikt:漢堡包">漢堡包</a></span>, <span lang="zh-Hans"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%B1%89%E5%A0%A1#Simplified_Chinese" title="wikt:汉堡">汉堡</a></span>/<span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%BC%A2%E5%A0%A1#Traditional_Chinese" title="wikt:漢堡">漢堡</a></span> 'hamburger'</li>
<li><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn">wǒ</i></span> <span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%88%91#Traditional_Chinese" title="wikt:我">我</a></span> 'I, me'</li>
<li><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn">shǒuményuán</i></span> <span lang="zh-Hans"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%AE%88%E9%97%A8%E5%91%98#Simplified_Chinese" title="wikt:守门员">守门员</a></span>/<span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%AE%88%E9%96%80%E5%93%A1#Traditional_Chinese" title="wikt:守門員">守門員</a></span> 'goalkeeper'</li>
<li><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn">rén</i></span> <span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E4%BA%BA#Traditional_Chinese" title="wikt:人">人</a></span> 'people, human, mankind'</li>
<li><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn">dìqiú</i></span> <span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%9C%B0%E7%90%83#Traditional_Chinese" title="wikt:地球">地球</a></span> 'The Earth'</li>
<li><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn">shǎndiàn</i></span> <span lang="zh-Hans"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%97%AA%E7%94%B5#Simplified_Chinese" title="wikt:闪电">闪电</a></span>/<span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%96%83%E9%9B%BB#Traditional_Chinese" title="wikt:閃電">閃電</a></span> 'lightning'</li>
<li><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn">mèng</i></span> <span lang="zh-Hans"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%A2%A6#Simplified_Chinese" title="wikt:梦">梦</a></span>/<span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%A4%A2#Traditional_Chinese" title="wikt:夢">夢</a></span> 'dream'</li></ul>
<p>All varieties of modern Chinese are <a href="/wiki/Analytic_language" title="Analytic language">analytic languages</a>, in that they depend on <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">syntax</a> (word order and sentence structure) rather than <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">morphology</a>—i.e., changes in form of a word—to indicate the word's function in a sentence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198810_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198810-79">[74]</a></sup> In other words, Chinese has very few <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grammatical_inflection" title="Grammatical inflection">grammatical inflections</a>—it possesses no <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_tense" title="Grammatical tense">tenses</a>, no <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grammatical_voice" title="Grammatical voice">voices</a>, no <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">numbers</a> (singular, plural; though there are plural markers, for example for personal pronouns), and only a few <a href="/wiki/Article_(grammar)" title="Article (grammar)">articles</a> (i.e., equivalents to "the, a, an" in English).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[f]</a></sup>
</p><p>They make heavy use of <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_particle" title="Grammatical particle">grammatical particles</a> to indicate <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_aspect" title="Grammatical aspect">aspect</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_mood" title="Grammatical mood">mood</a>. In Mandarin Chinese, this involves the use of particles like <i>le</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">了</span></span> (perfective), <i>hái</i> <span lang="zh-Hans">还</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">還</span> ('still'), <i>yǐjīng</i> <span lang="zh-Hans">已经</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">已經</span> ('already'), and so on.
</p><p>Chinese has a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subject%E2%80%93verb%E2%80%93object" title="Subject–verb–object">subject–verb–object</a> <a href="/wiki/Word_order" title="Word order">word order</a>, and like many other <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_East_Asia" title="Languages of East Asia">languages of East Asia</a>, makes frequent use of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Topic%E2%80%93comment" title="Topic–comment">topic–comment</a> construction to form sentences. Chinese also has an extensive system of <a href="/wiki/Classifier_(linguistics)" title="Classifier (linguistics)">classifiers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Measure_word" title="Measure word">measure words</a>, another trait shared with neighboring languages like <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean</a>. Other notable grammatical features common to all the spoken varieties of Chinese include the use of <a href="/wiki/Serial_verb_construction" title="Serial verb construction">serial verb construction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pro-drop_language" title="Pro-drop language">pronoun dropping</a> and the related <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Null_subject_language" title="Null subject language">subject dropping</a>.
</p><p>Although the grammars of the spoken varieties share many traits, they do possess differences.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary">Vocabulary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Vocabulary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The entire Chinese character corpus since antiquity comprises well over 50,000 characters, of which only roughly 10,000 are in use and only about 3,000 are frequently used in Chinese media and newspapers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[75]</a></sup> However Chinese characters should not be confused with Chinese words. Because most Chinese words are made up of two or more characters, there are many more Chinese words than characters. A more accurate equivalent for a Chinese character is the <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">morpheme</a>, as characters represent the smallest grammatical units with individual meanings in the Chinese language.
</p><p>Estimates of the total number of Chinese words and lexicalized phrases vary greatly. The <i><a href="/wiki/Hanyu_Da_Zidian" title="Hanyu Da Zidian">Hanyu Da Zidian</a></i>, a compendium of Chinese characters, includes 54,678 head entries for characters, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bone_oracle" title="Bone oracle">bone oracle</a> versions. The <i><a href="/wiki/Zhonghua_Zihai" title="Zhonghua Zihai">Zhonghua Zihai</a></i> (1994) contains 85,568 head entries for character definitions, and is the largest reference work based purely on character and its literary variants. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CC-CEDICT" title="CC-CEDICT">CC-CEDICT</a> project (2010) contains 97,404 contemporary entries including idioms, technology terms and names of political figures, businesses and products. The 2009 version of the Webster's Digital Chinese Dictionary (WDCD),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[76]</a></sup> based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CC-CEDICT" title="CC-CEDICT">CC-CEDICT</a>, contains over 84,000 entries.
</p><p>The most comprehensive pure linguistic Chinese-language dictionary, the 12-volume <i><a href="/wiki/Hanyu_Da_Cidian" title="Hanyu Da Cidian">Hanyu Da Cidian</a></i>, records more than 23,000 head Chinese characters and gives over 370,000 definitions. The 1999 revised <i><a href="/wiki/Cihai" title="Cihai">Cihai</a></i>, a multi-volume encyclopedic dictionary reference work, gives 122,836 vocabulary entry definitions under 19,485 Chinese characters, including proper names, phrases and common zoological, geographical, sociological, scientific and technical terms.
</p><p>The 7th (2016) edition of <i><a href="/wiki/Xiandai_Hanyu_Cidian" title="Xiandai Hanyu Cidian">Xiandai Hanyu Cidian</a></i>, an authoritative one-volume dictionary on modern standard Chinese language as used in <a href="/wiki/Mainland_China" title="Mainland China">mainland China</a>, has 13,000 head characters and defines 70,000 words.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Loanwords">Loanwords</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Loanwords">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Like any other language, Chinese has absorbed a sizable number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loanwords" title="Loanwords">loanwords</a> from other cultures. Most Chinese words are formed out of native Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Morphemes" title="Morphemes">morphemes</a>, including words describing imported objects and ideas. However, direct phonetic borrowing of foreign words has gone on since ancient times.
</p><p>Some early <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a> loanwords in Chinese have been proposed, notably <span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%9C%9C#Chinese" title="wikt:蜜">蜜</a></span> <i>mì</i> "honey", <span lang="zh-Hans"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E7%8B%AE#Simplified_Chinese" title="wikt:狮">狮</a></span>/<span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E7%8D%85#Traditional_Chinese" title="wikt:獅">獅</a></span> <i>shī</i> "lion," and perhaps also <span lang="zh-Hans"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%A9%AC#Simplified_Chinese" title="wikt:马">马</a></span>/<span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%A6%AC#Traditional_Chinese" title="wikt:馬">馬</a></span> <i>mǎ</i> "horse", <span lang="zh-Hans"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E7%8C%AA#Simplified_Chinese" title="wikt:猪">猪</a></span>/<span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%B1%AC#Traditional_Chinese" title="wikt:豬">豬</a></span> <i>zhū</i> "pig", <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">犬</span></span> <i>quǎn</i> "dog", and <span lang="zh-Hans"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%B9%85#Simplified_Chinese" title="wikt:鹅">鹅</a></span>/<span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%B5%9D#Traditional_Chinese" title="wikt:鵝">鵝</a></span> <i>é</i> "goose".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[g]</a></sup>
Ancient words borrowed from along the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> since <a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese" title="Old Chinese">Old Chinese</a> include <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">葡萄</span></span> <i>pútáo</i> "<a href="/wiki/Grape" title="Grape">grape</a>", <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">石榴</span></span> <i>shíliu</i>/<i>shíliú</i> "<a href="/wiki/Pomegranate" title="Pomegranate">pomegranate</a>" and <span lang="zh-Hans">狮子</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">獅子</span> <i>shīzi</i> "<a href="/wiki/Lion" title="Lion">lion</a>". Some words were borrowed from Buddhist scriptures, including <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">佛</span></span> <i>Fó</i> "Buddha" and <span lang="zh-Hans">菩萨</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">菩薩</span> <i>Púsà</i> "bodhisattva." Other words came from nomadic peoples to the north, such as <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">胡同</span></span> <i>hútòng</i> "<a href="/wiki/Hutong" title="Hutong">hutong</a>". Words borrowed from the peoples along the Silk Road, such as <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">葡萄</span></span> "grape," generally have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persian</a> etymologies. Buddhist terminology is generally derived from <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P%C4%81li" title="Pāli">Pāli</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liturgical_language" title="Liturgical language">liturgical languages</a> of <a href="/wiki/North_India" title="North India">North India</a>. Words borrowed from the nomadic tribes of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gobi" title="Gobi">Gobi</a>, Mongolian or northeast regions generally have <a href="/wiki/Altaic_languages" title="Altaic languages">Altaic</a> etymologies, such as <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">琵琶</span></span> <i>pípá</i>, the Chinese lute, or <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">酪</span></span> <i>lào</i>/<i>luò</i> "cheese" or "<a href="/wiki/Yogurt" title="Yogurt">yogurt</a>", but from exactly which source is not always clear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKane2006161_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKane2006161-84">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_borrowings">Modern borrowings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Modern borrowings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Translation_of_neologisms_into_Chinese" title="Translation of neologisms into Chinese">Translation of neologisms into Chinese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transcription_into_Chinese_characters" title="Transcription into Chinese characters">Transcription into Chinese characters</a></div>
<p>Modern neologisms are primarily translated into Chinese in one of three ways: free translation (<i><a href="/wiki/Calque" title="Calque">calque</a></i>, or by meaning), phonetic translation (by sound), or <a href="/wiki/Phono-semantic_matching" title="Phono-semantic matching">a combination of the two</a>. Today, it is much more common to use existing Chinese morphemes to coin new words to represent imported concepts, such as technical expressions and <a href="/wiki/International_scientific_vocabulary" title="International scientific vocabulary">international scientific vocabulary</a>. Any <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> or <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> etymologies are dropped and converted into the corresponding Chinese characters (for example, <i>anti-</i> typically becomes "<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">反</span></span>", literally <i>opposite</i>), making them more comprehensible for Chinese but introducing more difficulties in understanding foreign texts. For example, the word <i>telephone</i> was initially loaned phonetically as <span lang="zh-Hans">德律风</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">德律風</span> (Shanghainese: <i>télífon</i> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[təlɪfoŋ]</span>, Mandarin: <i>délǜfēng</i>) during the 1920s and widely used in Shanghai, but later <span lang="zh-Hans">电话</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">電話</span> <i>diànhuà</i> (lit. "electric speech"), built out of native Chinese morphemes, became prevalent (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">電話</span></span> is in fact from the Japanese <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">電話</span></span></span> <i>denwa</i>; see below for more Japanese loans). Other examples include <span lang="zh-Hans">电视</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">電視</span> <i>diànshì</i> (lit. "electric vision") for television, <span lang="zh-Hans">电脑</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">電腦</span> <i>diànnǎo</i> (lit. "electric brain") for computer; <span lang="zh-Hans">手机</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">手機</span> <i>shǒujī</i> (lit. "hand machine") for mobile phone, <span lang="zh-Hans">蓝牙</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">藍牙</span> <i>lányá</i> (lit. "blue tooth") for <a href="/wiki/Bluetooth" title="Bluetooth">Bluetooth</a>, and <span lang="zh-Hans">网志</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">網誌</span> <i>wǎngzhì</i> (lit. "internet logbook") for blog in Hong Kong and Macau Cantonese. Occasionally half-transliteration, half-translation compromises are accepted, such as <span lang="zh-Hans">汉堡包</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">漢堡包</span> <i>hànbǎobāo</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">漢堡</span></span> <i>hànbǎo</i> "Hamburg" + <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">包</span></span> <i>bāo</i> "bun") for "hamburger". Sometimes translations are designed so that they sound like the original while incorporating Chinese morphemes (<a href="/wiki/Phono-semantic_matching" title="Phono-semantic matching">phono-semantic matching</a>), such as <span lang="zh-Hans">马利奥</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">馬利奧</span> Mǎlì'ào for the video game character <a href="/wiki/Mario" title="Mario">Mario</a>. This is often done for commercial purposes, for example <span lang="zh-Hans">奔腾</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">奔騰</span> <i>bēnténg</i> (lit. "dashing-leaping") for <a href="/wiki/Pentium" title="Pentium">Pentium</a> and <span lang="zh-Hans">赛百味</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">賽百味</span> <i>Sàibǎiwèi</i> (lit. "better-than hundred tastes") for <a href="/wiki/Subway_(restaurant)" title="Subway (restaurant)">Subway restaurants</a>.
</p><p>Foreign words, mainly <a href="/wiki/Proper_noun" title="Proper noun">proper nouns</a>, continue to enter the Chinese language by transcription according to their pronunciations. This is done by employing Chinese characters with similar pronunciations. For example, "Israel" becomes <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">以色列</span></span> <i>Yǐsèliè</i>, "Paris" becomes <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">巴黎</span></span> <i>Bālí</i>. A rather small number of direct transliterations have survived as common words, including <span lang="zh-Hans">沙发</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">沙發</span> <i>shāfā</i> "sofa", <span lang="zh-Hans">马达</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">馬達</span> <i>mǎdá</i> "motor", <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">幽默</span></span> <i>yōumò</i> "humor", <span lang="zh-Hans">逻辑</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">邏輯</span> <i>luóji</i>/<i>luójí</i> "logic", <span lang="zh-Hans">时髦</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">時髦</span> <i>shímáo</i> "smart, fashionable", and <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">歇斯底里</span></span> <i>xiēsīdǐlǐ</i> "hysterics". The bulk of these words were originally coined in the Shanghai dialect during the early 20th century and were later loaned into Mandarin, hence their pronunciations in Mandarin may be quite off from the English. For example, <span lang="zh-Hans">沙发</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">沙發</span> "sofa" and <span lang="zh-Hans">马达</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">馬達</span> "motor" in Shanghainese sound more like their English counterparts. Cantonese differs from Mandarin with some transliterations, such as <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">梳化</span></span> <i>so<sup>1</sup> faa<sup>3*2</sup></i> "sofa" and <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">摩打</span></span> <i>mo<sup>1</sup> daa<sup>2</sup></i> "motor".
</p><p>Western foreign words representing Western concepts have influenced Chinese since the 20th century through transcription. From <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> came <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">芭蕾</span></span> <i>bālěi</i> "ballet" and <span lang="zh-Hans">香槟</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">香檳</span> <i>xiāngbīn</i>, "champagne"; from <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a>, <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">咖啡</span></span> <i>kāfēi</i> "caffè". English influence is particularly pronounced. From early 20th century Shanghainese, many English words are borrowed, such as <span lang="zh-Hans">高尔夫</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">高爾夫</span> <i>gāoěrfū</i> "golf" and the above-mentioned <span lang="zh-Hans">沙发</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">沙發</span> <i>shāfā</i> "sofa". Later, the United States <a href="/wiki/Soft_power" title="Soft power">soft influences</a> gave rise to <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">迪斯科</span></span> <i>dísikē</i>/<i>dísīkē</i> "disco", <span lang="zh-Hans">可乐</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">可樂</span> <i>kělè</i> "cola", and <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">迷你</span></span> <i>mínǐ</i> "mini [skirt]". Contemporary colloquial Cantonese has distinct loanwords from English, such as <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">卡通</span></span> <i>kaa<sup>1</sup> tung<small>1</small></i> "cartoon", <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">基佬</span></span> <i>gei<sup>1</sup> lou<sup>2</sup></i> "gay people", <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">的士</span></span> <i>dik<sup>1</sup> si<sup>6*2</sup></i> "taxi", and <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">巴士</span></span> <i>baa<sup>1</sup> si<sup>6*2</sup></i> "bus". With the rising popularity of the Internet, there is a current vogue in China for coining English transliterations, for example, <span lang="zh-Hans">粉丝</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">粉絲</span> <i>fěnsī</i> "fans", <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">黑客</span></span> <i>hēikè</i> "hacker" (lit. "black guest"), and <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">博客</span></span> <i>bókè</i> "blog". In Taiwan, some of these transliterations are different, such as <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">駭客</span></span> <i>hàikè</i> for "hacker" and <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">部落格</span></span> <i>bùluògé</i> for "blog" (lit. "interconnected tribes").
</p><p>Another result of the English influence on Chinese is the appearance in Modern Chinese texts of so-called <span lang="zh-Hans">字母词</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">字母詞</span> <i>zìmǔcí</i> (lit. "lettered words") spelled with letters from the English alphabet. This has appeared in magazines, newspapers, on web sites, and on TV: <span lang="zh-Hans">三G手机</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">三G手機</span> "3rd generation cell phones" (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">三</span></span> <i>sān</i> "three" + G "generation" + <span lang="zh-Hans">手机</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">手機</span> <i>shǒujī</i> "mobile phones"), <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">IT界</span></span> "IT circles" (IT "information technology" + <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">界</span></span> <i>jiè</i> "industry"), HSK (<i>Hànyǔ Shuǐpíng Kǎoshì</i>, <span lang="zh-Hans">汉语水平考试</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">漢語水平考試</span>), GB (<i>Guóbiāo</i>, <span lang="zh-Hans">国标</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">國標</span>), <span lang="zh-Hans">CIF价</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">CIF價</span> (CIF "Cost, Insurance, Freight" + <span lang="zh-Hans">价</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">價</span> <i>jià</i> "price"), <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">e家庭</span></span> "e-home" (e "electronic" + <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">家庭</span></span> <i>jiātíng</i> "home"), <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hans">W时代</span>/<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">W時代</span> "wireless era" (W "wireless" + <span lang="zh-Hans">时代</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">時代</span> <i>shídài</i> "era"), <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">TV族</span></span> "TV watchers" (TV "television" + <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">族</span></span> <i>zú</i> "social group; clan"), <span lang="zh-Hans">后РС时代</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">後PC時代</span> "post-PC era" (<span lang="zh-Hans">后</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">後</span> <i>hòu</i> "after/post-" + PC "personal computer" + <span lang="zh-Hans">时代</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">時代</span>), and so on.
</p><p>Since the 20th century, another source of words has been <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a> using existing <a href="/wiki/Kanji" title="Kanji">kanji</a> (Chinese characters used in Japanese). Japanese re-molded European concepts and inventions into <i><a href="/wiki/Wasei-kango" title="Wasei-kango">wasei-kango</a></i><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">和製漢語</span></span>, lit. "Japanese-made Chinese")</span>, and many of these words have been re-loaned into modern Chinese. Other terms were coined by the Japanese by giving new senses to existing Chinese terms or by referring to expressions used in classical Chinese literature. For example, <i>jīngjì</i> (<span lang="zh-Hans">经济</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">經濟</span>; <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">経済</span></span></span> <i>keizai</i> in Japanese), which in the original Chinese meant "the workings of the state", was narrowed to "economy" in Japanese; this narrowed definition was then <a href="/wiki/Wasei-kango" title="Wasei-kango">reimported</a> into Chinese. As a result, these terms are virtually indistinguishable from native Chinese words: indeed, there is some dispute over some of these terms as to whether the Japanese or Chinese coined them first. As a result of this loaning, Chinese, Korean, Japanese, and Vietnamese share a corpus of linguistic terms describing modern terminology, paralleling the similar corpus of terms built from Greco-Latin and shared among European languages.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing_system">Writing system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Writing system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:XingshuLantingxv.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="531" data-file-width="363" decoding="async" height="322" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/XingshuLantingxv.jpg/220px-XingshuLantingxv.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/XingshuLantingxv.jpg/330px-XingshuLantingxv.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1e/XingshuLantingxv.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:XingshuLantingxv.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"<a href="/wiki/Lantingji_Xu" title="Lantingji Xu">Preface to the Poems Composed at the Orchid Pavilion</a>" by <a href="/wiki/Wang_Xizhi" title="Wang Xizhi">Wang Xizhi</a>, written in <a href="/wiki/Semi-cursive_script" title="Semi-cursive script">semi-cursive style</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Written_Chinese" title="Written Chinese">Written Chinese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mainland_Chinese_Braille" title="Mainland Chinese Braille">Mainland Chinese Braille</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_Braille" title="Taiwanese Braille">Taiwanese Braille</a></div>
<p>The Chinese <a href="/wiki/Orthography" title="Orthography">orthography</a> centers on <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese characters</a>, which are written within imaginary square blocks, traditionally arranged in vertical columns, read from top to bottom down a column, and right to left across columns, despite alternative arrangement with rows of characters from left to right within a row and from top to bottom across rows (like English and other Western writing systems) having become more popular since the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[78]</a></sup> Chinese characters denote <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">morphemes</a> independent of phonetic variation in different languages. Thus the character <span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E4%B8%80#Chinese" title="wikt:一">一</a></span> ("one") is uttered <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">yī</i></span> in <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Chinese</a>, <span title="Yue Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="yue-Latn">yat<sup>1</sup></i></span> in <a href="/wiki/Cantonese" title="Cantonese">Cantonese</a> and <i>it</i> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hokkien_dialect" title="Hokkien dialect">Hokkien</a> (form of Min).
</p><p>Most written Chinese documents in the modern time, especially the more formal ones, are created using the grammar and syntax of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_Mandarin_Chinese" title="Standard Mandarin Chinese">Standard Mandarin Chinese</a> variants, regardless of dialectical background of the author or targeted audience. This replaced the old writing language standard of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Literary_Chinese" title="Literary Chinese">Literary Chinese</a> before the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[79]</a></sup> However, vocabularies from different Chinese-speaking areas have diverged, and the divergence can be observed in written Chinese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>Meanwhile, colloquial forms of various Chinese language variants have also been written down by their users, especially in less formal settings. The most prominent example of this is the <a href="/wiki/Written_Cantonese" title="Written Cantonese">written colloquial form of Cantonese</a>, which has become quite popular in <a href="/wiki/Tabloid_journalism" title="Tabloid journalism">tabloids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Instant_messaging" title="Instant messaging">instant messaging</a> applications, and on the internet amongst Hong-Kongers and Cantonese-speakers elsewhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because some Chinese variants have diverged and developed a number of unique morphemes that are not found in Standard Mandarin (despite all other common morphemes), unique characters rarely used in Standard Chinese have also been created or inherited from archaic literary standard to represent these unique morphemes. For example, characters like <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">冇</span></span> and <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">係</span></span> for Cantonese and <a href="/wiki/Hakka_Chinese" title="Hakka Chinese">Hakka</a>, are actively used in both languages while being considered archaic or unused in standard written Chinese.
</p><p>The Chinese had no uniform phonetic transcription system for most of its speakers until the mid-20th century, although enunciation patterns were recorded in early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rime_book" title="Rime book">rime books</a> and dictionaries. Early <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">Indian</a> translators, working in <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pali" title="Pali">Pali</a>, were the first to attempt to describe the sounds and enunciation patterns of Chinese in a foreign language. After the 15th century, the efforts of Jesuits and Western court missionaries resulted in some Latin character transcription/writing systems, based on various variants of Chinese languages. Some of these Latin character based systems are still being used to write various Chinese variants in the modern era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Hunan" title="Hunan">Hunan</a>, women in certain areas write their local Chinese language variant in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N%C3%BC_Shu" title="Nü Shu">Nü Shu</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Syllabary" title="Syllabary">syllabary</a> derived from <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese characters</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Dungan_language" title="Dungan language">Dungan language</a>, considered by many a dialect of Mandarin, is nowadays written in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyrillic" title="Cyrillic">Cyrillic</a>, and was previously written in the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_script" title="Arabic script">Arabic script</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Dungan_people" title="Dungan people">Dungan people</a> are primarily Muslim and live mainly in <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyzstan" title="Kyrgyzstan">Kyrgyzstan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>; some of the related <a href="/wiki/Hui_people" title="Hui people">Hui people</a> also speak the language and live mainly in China.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chinese_characters">Chinese characters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Chinese characters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:8_strokes_of_%E6%B0%B8-zh.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="625" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/8_strokes_of_%E6%B0%B8-zh.svg/220px-8_strokes_of_%E6%B0%B8-zh.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/8_strokes_of_%E6%B0%B8-zh.svg/330px-8_strokes_of_%E6%B0%B8-zh.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/8_strokes_of_%E6%B0%B8-zh.svg/440px-8_strokes_of_%E6%B0%B8-zh.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:8_strokes_of_%E6%B0%B8-zh.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>永 (meaning "forever") is often used to illustrate the eight basic types of strokes of Chinese characters.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese characters</a></div>
<p>Each Chinese character represents a monosyllabic Chinese word or morpheme. In 100 CE, the famed <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> scholar <a href="/wiki/Xu_Shen" title="Xu Shen">Xu Shen</a> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_character_classification" title="Chinese character classification">classified characters</a> into six categories, namely <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pictograph" title="Pictograph">pictographs</a>, simple <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ideograph" title="Ideograph">ideographs</a>, compound ideographs, phonetic loans, phonetic compounds and derivative characters. Of these, only 4% were categorized as pictographs, including many of the simplest characters, such as <i>rén</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">人</span></span> (human), <i>rì</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">日</span></span> (sun), <i>shān</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">山</span></span> (mountain; hill), <i>shuǐ</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">水</span></span> (water). Between 80% and 90% were classified as phonetic compounds such as <i>chōng</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">沖</span></span> (pour), combining a phonetic component <i>zhōng</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">中</span></span> (middle) with a semantic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radical_(Chinese_character)" title="Radical (Chinese character)">radical</a> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">氵</span></span> (water). Almost all characters created since have been made using this format. The 18th-century <a href="/wiki/Kangxi_Dictionary" title="Kangxi Dictionary">Kangxi Dictionary</a> recognized 214 radicals.
</p><p>Modern characters are styled after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kaishu" title="Kaishu">regular script</a>. Various other written styles are also used in <a href="/wiki/Chinese_calligraphy" title="Chinese calligraphy">Chinese calligraphy</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Seal_script" title="Seal script">seal script</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cursive_script_(East_Asia)" title="Cursive script (East Asia)">cursive script</a> and <a href="/wiki/Clerical_script" title="Clerical script">clerical script</a>. Calligraphy artists can write in traditional and simplified characters, but they tend to use traditional characters for traditional art.
</p><p>There are currently two systems for Chinese characters. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_character" title="Traditional Chinese character">traditional system</a>, used in <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">Macau</a> and Chinese speaking communities (except <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>) outside <a href="/wiki/Mainland_China" title="Mainland China">mainland China</a>, takes its form from standardized character forms dating back to the late Han dynasty. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_character" title="Simplified Chinese character">Simplified Chinese character</a> system, introduced by the People's Republic of China in 1954 to promote mass <a href="/wiki/Literacy" title="Literacy">literacy</a>, simplifies most complex traditional <a href="/wiki/Glyph" title="Glyph">glyphs</a> to fewer strokes, many to common cursive <a href="/wiki/Shorthand" title="Shorthand">shorthand</a> variants. <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>, which has a large Chinese community, was the second nation to officially adopt simplified characters, although it has also become the <i>de facto</i> standard for younger ethnic Chinese in <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a> provides the platform to practice reading these alternative systems, be it traditional or simplified. Most Chinese users in the modern era are capable of, although not necessarily comfortable with, reading (but not writing) the alternative system, through experience and guesswork.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>A well-educated Chinese reader today recognizes approximately 4,000 to 6,000 characters; approximately 3,000 characters are required to read a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newspapers_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="Newspapers of the People's Republic of China">Mainland newspaper</a>. The PRC government defines literacy amongst workers as a knowledge of 2,000 characters, though this would be only functional literacy. School-children typically learn around 2,000 characters whereas scholars may memorize up to 10,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[84]</a></sup> A large unabridged <a href="/wiki/Dictionary" title="Dictionary">dictionary</a>, like the Kangxi Dictionary, contains over 40,000 characters, including obscure, variant, rare, and archaic characters; fewer than a quarter of these characters are now commonly used.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Romanization">Romanization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Romanization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Chinese" title="Romanization of Chinese">Romanization of Chinese</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gwoyu.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="660" data-file-width="320" decoding="async" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Gwoyu.svg/100px-Gwoyu.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Gwoyu.svg/150px-Gwoyu.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Gwoyu.svg/200px-Gwoyu.svg.png 2x" width="100"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gwoyu.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"National language" (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">國語</span></span>/<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hans">国语</span></span>; <i>Guóyǔ</i>) written in Traditional and Simplified Chinese characters, followed by various romanizations.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Romanization" title="Romanization">Romanization</a> is the process of transcribing a language into the <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin script</a>. There are many systems of romanization for the Chinese varieties, due to the lack of a native phonetic transcription until modern times. Chinese is first known to have been written in Latin characters by Western <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_China" title="Christianity in China">Christian missionaries</a> in the 16th century.
</p><p>Today the most common romanization standard for Standard Mandarin is <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hanyu_Pinyin" title="Hanyu Pinyin">Hanyu Pinyin</a></i>, often known simply as pinyin, introduced in 1956 by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China">People's Republic of China</a>, and later adopted by <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>. Pinyin is almost universally employed now for teaching standard spoken Chinese in schools and universities across the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>. Chinese parents also use Pinyin to teach their children the sounds and tones of new words. In school books that teach Chinese, the Pinyin romanization is often shown below a picture of the thing the word represents, with the Chinese character alongside.
</p><p>The second-most common romanization system, the <a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="Wade–Giles">Wade–Giles</a>, was invented by Thomas Wade in 1859 and modified by Herbert Giles in 1892. As this system approximates the phonology of Mandarin Chinese into English consonants and vowels, i.e. it is largely an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglicization" title="Anglicization">Anglicization</a>, it may be particularly helpful for beginner Chinese speakers of an English-speaking background. Wade–Giles was found in academic use in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, particularly before the 1980s, and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language_romanization_in_Taiwan" title="Chinese language romanization in Taiwan">until 2009</a> was widely used in Taiwan.
</p><p>When used within European texts, the <a href="/wiki/Tone_(linguistics)" title="Tone (linguistics)">tone</a> transcriptions in both pinyin and Wade–Giles are often left out for simplicity; Wade–Giles' extensive use of apostrophes is also usually omitted. Thus, most Western readers will be much more familiar with <i>Beijing</i> than they will be with <i>Běijīng</i> (pinyin), and with <i>Taipei</i> than <i>T'ai²-pei³</i> (Wade–Giles). This simplification presents syllables as homophones which really are none, and therefore exaggerates the number of homophones almost by a factor of four.
</p><p>Here are a few examples of <i>Hanyu Pinyin</i> and Wade–Giles, for comparison:
</p>

</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Other systems of romanization for Chinese include <a href="/wiki/Gwoyeu_Romatzyh" title="Gwoyeu Romatzyh">Gwoyeu Romatzyh</a>, the French <a href="/wiki/EFEO_Chinese_transcription" title="EFEO Chinese transcription">EFEO</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Yale_romanization_of_Mandarin" title="Yale romanization of Mandarin">Yale system</a> (invented during WWII for U.S. troops), as well as separate systems for <a href="/wiki/Cantonese" title="Cantonese">Cantonese</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Min_Nan" title="Min Nan">Min Nan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hakka_Chinese" title="Hakka Chinese">Hakka</a>, and other Chinese varieties.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_phonetic_transcriptions">Other phonetic transcriptions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Other phonetic transcriptions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Chinese varieties have been phonetically transcribed into many other writing systems over the centuries. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%27Phags-pa_script" title="'Phags-pa script">'Phags-pa script</a>, for example, has been very helpful in reconstructing the pronunciations of premodern forms of Chinese.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhuyin" title="Zhuyin">Zhuyin</a> (colloquially <i>bopomofo</i>), a <a href="/wiki/Semi-syllabary" title="Semi-syllabary">semi-syllabary</a> is still widely used in Taiwan's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elementary_school" title="Elementary school">elementary schools</a> to aid standard pronunciation. Although zhuyin characters are reminiscent of <a href="/wiki/Katakana" title="Katakana">katakana</a> script, there is no source to substantiate the claim that Katakana was the basis for the zhuyin system. A comparison table of zhuyin to pinyin exists in the <a href="/wiki/Bopomofo#Comparison" title="Bopomofo">zhuyin article</a>. Syllables based on pinyin and zhuyin can also be compared by looking at the following articles:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Pinyin_table" title="Pinyin table">Pinyin table</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Zhuyin_table" title="Zhuyin table">Zhuyin table</a></li></ul>
<p>There are also at least two systems of <a href="/wiki/Cyrillization" title="Cyrillization">cyrillization</a> for Chinese. The most widespread is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyrillization_of_Chinese_from_Pinyin" title="Cyrillization of Chinese from Pinyin">Palladius system</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="As_a_foreign_language">As a foreign language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: As a foreign language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_as_a_foreign_language" title="Chinese as a foreign language">Chinese as a foreign language</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_Language_Training_at_CASA.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="651" data-file-width="987" decoding="async" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Chinese_Language_Training_at_CASA.PNG/300px-Chinese_Language_Training_at_CASA.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Chinese_Language_Training_at_CASA.PNG/450px-Chinese_Language_Training_at_CASA.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Chinese_Language_Training_at_CASA.PNG/600px-Chinese_Language_Training_at_CASA.PNG 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_Language_Training_at_CASA.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Yang Lingfu, former curator of the <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_China" title="National Museum of China">National Museum of China</a>, giving Chinese language instruction at the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Affairs_Staging_Area" title="Civil Affairs Staging Area">Civil Affairs Staging Area</a> in 1945.</div></div></div>
<p>With the growing importance and influence of China's economy globally, <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Mandarin</a> instruction has been gaining popularity in schools throughout East Asia, Southeast Asia, and the Western world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>Besides Mandarin, <a href="/wiki/Cantonese" title="Cantonese">Cantonese</a> is the only other Chinese language that is widely taught as a foreign language, largely due to the economic and cultural influence of Hong Kong and its widespread usage among significant Overseas Chinese communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1991 there were 2,000 foreign learners taking China's official <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_Proficiency_Test" title="Chinese Proficiency Test">Chinese Proficiency Test</a> (also known as HSK, comparable to the English <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge_ESOL_examination" title="University of Cambridge ESOL examination">Cambridge Certificate</a>), then the number of candidates had risen sharply to 117,660 in 2005<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[87]</a></sup> and 750,000 in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[88]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>De facto</i> – while no specific variety of Chinese is official in Hong Kong and Macau, Cantonese is the predominant spoken form and the <i><a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a></i> regional standard, written in <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese characters</a>. <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Mandarin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese characters</a> are only occasionally used in some official and educational settings. The HK SAR Government promotes 兩文三語 [Bi-literacy (Chinese, English) and Tri-lingualism (Cantonese, Mandarin, English)], while the Macau SAR Government promotes 三文四語 [Tri-literacy (Chinese, Portuguese, English) and Quad-lingualism (Cantonese, Mandarin, Portuguese, English)], especially in public education.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><abbr title="literal translation">lit.</abbr> "<a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han</a> language"</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><abbr title="literal translation">lit.</abbr> "Chinese writing"</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Various examples include:
<ul><li>David Crystal, <i>The Cambridge Encyclopedia of Language</i> (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1987), p. 312. "The mutual unintelligibility of the varieties is the main ground for referring to them as separate languages."</li>
<li>Charles N. Li, Sandra A. Thompson. <i>Mandarin Chinese: A Functional Reference Grammar</i> (1989), p. 2. "The Chinese language family is genetically classified as an independent branch of the Sino-Tibetan language family."</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFNorman1988">Norman (1988)</a>, p. 1. "[...] the modern Chinese dialects are really more like a family of languages [...]"</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFDeFrancis1984">DeFrancis (1984)</a>, p. 56. "To call Chinese a single language composed of dialects with varying degrees of difference is to mislead by minimizing disparities that according to Chao are as great as those between English and Dutch. To call Chinese a family of languages is to suggest extralinguistic differences that in fact do not exist and to overlook the unique linguistic situation that exists in China."</li></ul>
Linguists in China often use a formulation introduced by <a href="/wiki/Fu_Maoji" title="Fu Maoji">Fu Maoji</a> in the <i><a href="/wiki/Encyclopedia_of_China" title="Encyclopedia of China">Encyclopedia of China</a></i>: "<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">汉语在语言系属分类中相当于一个语族的地位。</span></span>" ("In language classification, Chinese has a status equivalent to a language family.")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMair199110,_21_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMair199110,_21-5">[2]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-DeFrancis_p42-75"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-DeFrancis_p42_75-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-DeFrancis_p42_75-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFDeFrancis1984">DeFrancis (1984)</a>, p. 42 counts Chinese as having 1,277 tonal syllables, and about 398 to 418 if tones are disregarded; he cites Jespersen, Otto (1928) <i>Monosyllabism in English</i>; London, p. 15 for a count of over 8000 syllables for English.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-80"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-80">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A distinction is made between <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">他</span></span> as 'he' and <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">她</span></span> as 'she' in writing, but this is a 20th-century introduction, and both characters are pronounced in exactly the same way.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-83"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-83">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica" title="Encyclopædia Britannica">Encyclopædia Britannica</a> s.v. "<a class="external text" href="https://www.britannica.com/eb/article-75039/Chinese-languages" rel="nofollow">Chinese languages</a>": "Old Chinese vocabulary already contained many words not generally occurring in the other Sino-Tibetan languages. The words for 'honey' and 'lion', and probably also 'horse', 'dog', and 'goose', are connected with Indo-European and were acquired through trade and early contacts. (The nearest known Indo-European languages were Tocharian and Sogdian, a middle Iranian language.) A number of words have Austroasiatic cognates and point to early contacts with the ancestral language of Muong–Vietnamese and Mon–Khmer."; Jan Ulenbrook, <i>Einige Übereinstimmungen zwischen dem Chinesischen und dem Indogermanischen</i> (1967) proposes 57 items; see also Tsung-tung Chang, 1988 <a class="external text" href="http://sino-platonic.org/complete/spp007_old_chinese.pdf" rel="nofollow">Indo-European Vocabulary in Old Chinese</a>.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li><a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Sign_Language" title="Tibetan Sign Language">Tibetan Sign</a><b><sup>XZ</sup></b></li></ul>
